                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";

                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome assembly with in vitro proximity ligation data and whole-genome triplication in lettuce"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2017 8:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-04-12"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Lettuce (Lactuca sativa) is a major crop and a member of the large, highly successful Compositae family of flowering plants. Here we present a reference assembly for the species and family. This was generated using whole-genome shotgun Illumina reads plus in vitro proximity ligation data to create large superscaffolds; it was validated genetically and superscaffolds were oriented in genetic bins ordered along nine chromosomal pseudomolecules. We identify several genomic features that may have contributed to the success of the family, including genes encoding Cycloidea-like transcription factors, kinases, enzymes involved in rubber biosynthesis and disease resistance proteins that are expanded in the genome. We characterize 21 novel microRNAs, one of which may trigger phasiRNAs from numerous kinase transcripts. We provide evidence for a whole-genome triplication event specific but basal to the Compositae. We detect 26% of the genome in triplicated regions containing 30% of all genes that are enriched for regulatory sequences and depleted for genes involved in defence. Genome assembly for many plant species can be challenging due to large size and high repeat content. Here, the authors usein vitroproximity ligation to assemble the genome of lettuce, revealing a family-specific triplication event and providing a comprehensive reference genome for a member of the Compositae."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Lettuce (<i>Lactuca sativa</i>) is a major crop and a member of the large, highly successful Compositae family of flowering plants. Here we present a reference assembly for the species and family. This was generated using whole-genome shotgun Illumina reads plus <i>in vitro</i> proximity ligation data to create large superscaffolds; it was validated genetically and superscaffolds were oriented in genetic bins ordered along nine chromosomal pseudomolecules. We identify several genomic features that may have contributed to the success of the family, including genes encoding <i>Cycloidea</i>-like transcription factors, kinases, enzymes involved in rubber biosynthesis and disease resistance proteins that are expanded in the genome. We characterize 21 novel microRNAs, one of which may trigger phasiRNAs from numerous kinase transcripts. We provide evidence for a whole-genome triplication event specific but basal to the Compositae. We detect 26% of the genome in triplicated regions containing 30% of all genes that are enriched for regulatory sequences and depleted for genes involved in defence.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The Compositae (also known as Asteraceae) is the most successful family of flowering plants on earth in terms of number of species and diversity of habitats colonized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Funk, V. A. et al. in Plant Diversity and Complexity Patterns: Local, Regional, and Global Dimensions: Proc. Int. Symp. Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters in Copenhagen, Denmark, 25–28 May 2003, Vol. 55, (eds Friis, I. &amp; Balslev, H.) 343–374 (Kgl. Danske Videnskabernes Selskab, 2005)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d2878e752">1</a></sup>. The family is thought to have originated in the mid-Eocene (45–49 Myr) and expanded greatly during the Oligocene (28–36 Myr)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Kim, K.-J., Choi, K.-S. &amp; Jansen, R. K. Two chloroplast DNA inversions originated simultaneously during the early evolution of the Sunflower family (Asteraceae). Mol. Biol. Evol. 22, 1783–1792 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d2878e756">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Barreda, V. D. et al. Eocene Patagonia fossils of the Daisy family. Science 329, 1621 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2878e759">3</a></sup>. It encompasses 1,620 recognized genera and at least 23,600 species, constituting approximately 10% of all angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Funk, V. A. et al. in Plant Diversity and Complexity Patterns: Local, Regional, and Global Dimensions: Proc. Int. Symp. Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters in Copenhagen, Denmark, 25–28 May 2003, Vol. 55, (eds Friis, I. &amp; Balslev, H.) 343–374 (Kgl. Danske Videnskabernes Selskab, 2005)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d2878e763">1</a></sup>. They are easily recognizable by their characteristic compound inflorescences that comprise many true flowers. This cosmopolitan family is present in diverse habitats; Compositae species are successful colonizers of disturbed habitats and thrive in a range of extreme environments including deserts, tundra and salt flats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Funk, V. A. et al. in Plant Diversity and Complexity Patterns: Local, Regional, and Global Dimensions: Proc. Int. Symp. Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters in Copenhagen, Denmark, 25–28 May 2003, Vol. 55, (eds Friis, I. &amp; Balslev, H.) 343–374 (Kgl. Danske Videnskabernes Selskab, 2005)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d2878e767">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Judd, W. S., Campbell, C. S., Kellogg, E. A., Stevens, P. F. &amp; Donoghue, M. J. Plant systematics: a phylogenetic approach. Ecol. Mediterr. 25, 215 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d2878e770">4</a></sup>. Although it does not contain any of the top six food crops, the family includes many important edible, medicinal, noxious and invasive species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Kesseli, R. V. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Genome Mapping in Plants. 179–191 (RG Landes Co., Georg, 1997)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d2878e774">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dempewolf, H., Rieseberg, L. &amp; Cronk, Q. Crop domestication in the Compositae: a family-wide trait assessment. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 55, 1141–1157 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2878e777">6</a></sup>. Over 200 species have been domesticated for a wide variety of uses. In aggregate, over 27 million hectares are planted worldwide to Compositae crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="FAO. FAOSTAT. FAOSTAT 
                    http://faostat3.fao.org/home/E
                    
                   (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d2878e782">7</a></sup>, of which the two most important are lettuce and sunflower. Medicinal species include <i>Artemesia</i> spp. and Echinacea. Many ornamental species with their showy flowers belong to the Compositae. In addition, numerous troublesome weeds such as star thistle are also Composites. Only two draft sequences of Compositae species have been published so far. One is of the small 335 Mb genome of horseweed (<i>Conyza canadensis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Peng, Y. et al. De novo genome assembly of the economically important weed horseweed using integrated data from multiple sequencing platforms. Plant Physiol. 166, 1241–1254 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2878e792">8</a></sup>; the second is of the 1 Gb genome of globe artichoke (<i>Cynara cardunculus</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Scaglione, D. et al. The genome sequence of the outbreeding globe artichoke constructed de novo incorporating a phase-aware low-pass sequencing strategy of F1 progeny. Sci. Rep 6, 19427 (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2878e799">9</a></sup>.</p><p>Lettuce (<i>Lactuca sativa</i> L.) is an important vegetable crop species and ranks as one of the top 10 most valuable crops in the USA with an annual value of over $2.4 billion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Anonymous. Crop Values: 201 4 Summary. USDA, 
                    http://usda.mannlib.cornell.edu/MannUsda/homepage.do
                    
                   (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d2878e809">10</a></sup>. <i>L. sativa</i> is diploid with 2<i>n</i>=2<i>x</i>=18 chromosomes and an estimated genome size of 2.5 Gb (refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Michaelson, M. J., Price, H. J., Ellison, J. R. &amp; Johnston, J. S. Comparison of plant DNA contents determined by Feulgen microspectrophotometry and laser flow cytometry. Am. J. Bot. 78, 183–188 (1991)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d2878e822">11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Koopman, W. J. M. &amp; De Jong, J. H. A numerical analysis of karyotypes and DNA amounts in lettuce cultivars and species (Lactuca subsect. Lactuca, Compositae). Acta Bot. Neerl. 45, 211–222 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d2878e825">12</a>). Generation of a comprehensive reference genome was challenging due to its size and high repeat content. We sequenced and assembled the genome of <i>L. sativa</i> using a variety of approaches that included a wide range of mate-pair libraries and <i>in vitro</i> proximity ligation to generate large superscaffolds based on long-range contact frequencies detected between scaffolds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res. 26, 342–350 (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d2878e835">13</a></sup>. <i>In vitro</i> proximity ligation is an application of the chromosome conformation capture technologies to aid genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Flot, J.-F., Marie-Nelly, H. &amp; Koszul, R. Contact genomics: scaffolding and phasing (meta)genomes using chromosome 3D physical signatures. FEBS Lett. 589, 2966–2974 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2878e843">14</a></sup>; this approach has been reported for animal but not for plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Rice, E. S. et al. Improved genome assembly of American alligator genome reveals conserved architecture of estrogen signaling. Preprint at bioRxiv, 
                    http://biorxiv.org/content/early/2016/08/01/067165
                    
                   (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d2878e847">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Session, A. M. et al. Genome evolution in the allotetraploid frog Xenopus laevis. Nature 538, 336–343 (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d2878e850">16</a></sup>. The assembly was validated genetically and superscaffolds were arranged in genetic bins ordered along the nine chromosomal linkage groups. The resulting assembly is one of the more complete for any plant species reported so far, particularly for a genome larger than 2 Gb with a high repeat content, and provides the first high-quality, comprehensive reference genome for analysis of the Compositae family.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>A whole-genome shotgun strategy was used to sequence and assemble the genome of <i>L. sativa</i> cultivar Salinas from Illumina short reads. A total of 198.5 Gb Illumina paired-end and mate-pair reads were generated from seven libraries of different fragment sizes (170 bp to 40 kb; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Table 1</a>). After filtering, this provided 72.5-fold coverage of the 2.7 Gb genome as estimated by K-mer analysis, making this one of the larger plant genomes assembled so far<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Michael, T. P. &amp; VanBuren, R. Progress, challenges and the future of crop genomes. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 24, 71–81 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d2878e872">17</a></sup>. An additional 47 Gb of paired-end reads from gene space libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Matvienko, M. et al. Consequences of normalizing transcriptomic and genomic libraries of plant genomes using a duplex-specific nuclease and tetramethylammonium chloride. PLoS ONE 8, e55913 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d2878e876">18</a></sup> were used when filling gaps. This initial SOAPdenovo assembly consisted of 153,952 contigs and 21,686 scaffolds greater than 1 kb with the largest scaffold being 3.1 Mb. The N<sub>50</sub>s of contigs and scaffolds were 12 and 476 kb, respectively. The mean size of gaps in the scaffolds was 1.3 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The Chicago library data (<i>in vitro</i> proximity ligation) scaffolded with the HiRise software pipeline (Dovetail Genomics LLC, Santa Cruz, CA, USA) increased the contiguity of scaffolds considerably (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Table 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). The final HiRise assembly decreased the 21,686 scaffolds to 11,474 superscaffolds and increased the N<sub>50</sub> from 476 to 1,769 kb; 50 and 90% of the genome is represented in only 385 and 1,520 superscaffolds, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The largest superscaffold is 12.2 Mb and contains 27 SOAPdenovo scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). The total length of the assembly is 2.38 Gb, covering <span class="stix">∼</span>88% of the estimated genome size of <i>L. sativa</i>. This assembly represents the gene space well; CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d2878e910">19</a></sup> analysis found 97.6% matches to the set of 248 Conserved Eukaryotic Genes (CEGs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d2878e914">19</a></sup>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Table 4</a>) and all 357 Ultra-Conserved Orthologs from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> were found using TBLASTN (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Table 4</a>). In addition, 96.6% of the 80,727 <i>L. sativa</i> expressed sequence tags (ESTs) in NCBI could be aligned to the genome assembly at &gt;80% identity and &gt;50% coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Table 4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Improvement of the L. sativa cv. Salinas genome assembly after scaffolding with HiRise."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Improvement of the <i>L. sativa</i> cv. Salinas genome assembly after scaffolding with HiRise.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms14953/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms14953/MediaObjects/41467_2017_Article_BFncomms14953_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms14953/MediaObjects/41467_2017_Article_BFncomms14953_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="523"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Higher contiguity of the genome assembly by scaffold using HiRise as a function of an increase in the sequence size and proportion of large sequences. (<b>b</b>) Genotype calls for RILs with crossovers across the longest HiRise superscaffold. Red bars represent <i>L. sativa</i> alleles, blue bars represent <i>L. serriola</i> alleles and the yellow bars represent heterozygotes. Alternating discontinuities in the black line of top of the genotype represent joins between SOAPdenovo scaffolds. (<b>c</b>) Ordering, orientation and incorporation of additional scaffolds spanning the Major Resistance Cluster 2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Christopoulou, M. et al. Genome-wide architecture of disease resistance genes in Lettuce. G3 Genes Genomes Genetics 5, 2655–2669 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d2878e965">31</a>) in the two HiRise assemblies. Expanded view shows a single super scaffold that organized four genetic bins into a single unit.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms14953/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly statistics for the genome of <i>L. sativa</i> cv. Salinas.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms14953/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Validation and anchoring of the assembly to linkage groups</h3><p>To validate the assembly and generate chromosomal pseudomolecules for <i>L</i>. <i>sativa</i>, we sequenced the gene space of 99 F<sub>7</sub> recombinant inbred lines (RILs) from an interspecific cross of <i>L. sativa</i> cv. Salinas <i> × L. serriola</i> acc. US96UC23 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Truco, M. J. et al. An ultra high-density, transcript-based, genetic map of Lettuce. G3 Genes Genomes Genetics 3, 617–631 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1515">20</a>). Haplotypes could be assigned to 12,023 of the larger scaffolds. The SOAPdenovo assembly was validated using both genetic and HiRise information. Only 171 (0.78%) of the scaffolds were identified as chimeric based on the presence of indicative population-wide switches in genotype calls occurring at the point of misjoin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Another 74 (0.34%) were identified as chimeric based in discrepancies in the contact frequency along the scaffold. In total, only 245 (1.13%) scaffolds were chimeric and had to be split reflecting the high quality of the initial SOAPdenovo assembly.</p><p>A total of 9,140 scaffolds of the <i>L. sativa</i> assembly could be clustered into nine chromosomal linkage groups and then mapped into genetic bins ordered along each chromosomal linkage group (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3169">Supplementary Data 2</a>). The groups were named and oriented consistent with the previously reported, ultra-dense map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Truco, M. J. et al. An ultra high-density, transcript-based, genetic map of Lettuce. G3 Genes Genomes Genetics 3, 617–631 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1534">20</a></sup> using genic sequences common to both maps. As expected, there was almost perfect colinearity between the map generated from segregation data resulting from hybridizations to the Affymetrix GeneChip and the map generated from the sequence data. Out of a total of 9,140 mapped scaffolds, less than 1% had inconsistent positions in the two maps; these inconsistencies may have been due to mapping of paralogs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><p>The combined genetic and HiRise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res. 26, 342–350 (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1544">13</a></sup> data provided further validation of the assembly, captured an additional 3,638 previously unmapped scaffolds that encompassed 49.5 Mb and refined the order and orientation of scaffolds in each genetic bin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). Genetic data identified only 24 (0.21%) misassembled superscaffolds. After splitting chimeric scaffolds, nine chromosomal pseudomolecules were generated and displayed using GBrowse (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>) (<a href="http://lgr.genomecenter.ucdavis.edu">http://lgr.genomecenter.ucdavis.edu</a>) with 3,138 superscaffolds encompassing 2.30 Gb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3170">Supplementary Data 3</a>) (96.6% of the assembly). The HiRise data reduced the number of sequences from an average of 6.26 scaffolds per bin to 2.25 superscaffolds per bin and resulted in reorganization and reorientation of scaffolds within genetic bins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>, Track a and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3171">Supplementary Data 4</a>). Of 3,112 superscaffolds that contained mapped fragments, 2,325 (74.7%) were located within a single genetic bin or had only a single mapped marker and so could not be oriented. The remaining 787 superscaffolds spanned on average 1.7 cM. Orientation could be determined for 859 superscaffolds based on crossovers within them (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>); this accurately placed 1.5 Gb (63%) of the assembly in the precise location and orientation. Such improvements are exemplified by the Major Resistance Cluster 2 region where the number of sequences was decreased by a third for this complex region that contains large numbers of paralogs encoding candidate-resistance proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). This improvement was also evident in the analysis of synteny on the genomic scale. We were able to detect twice the number of syntelogs in the final chromosomal pseudomolecules based on HiRise superscaffolds (9,325 syntenic hits) compared to the chromosomal pseudomolecules build with the SOAPDenovo scaffolds (4,038 syntenic hits) due to a significant improvement in gene placement. Telomeric sequence arrays were detected within the terminal bins of seven pseudomolecules. Five chromosomes show single regions of elevated scaffold density on a genetic basis indicative of the likely position of the centromeres (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>, Track a).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Overview of the L. sativa cv. Salinas genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Overview of the <i>L. sativa</i> cv. Salinas genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms14953/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms14953/MediaObjects/41467_2017_Article_BFncomms14953_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms14953/MediaObjects/41467_2017_Article_BFncomms14953_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="685"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Number of scaffolds in 1 Mb intervals indicating the greater contiguity with the HiRise analysis. Blue, SOAPdenovo scaffolds; red, HiRise superscaffolds. (<b>b</b>) Chromosomal pseudomolecules. Dark areas indicate the 63% of the genome that is positioned and oriented accurately. (<b>c</b>) Gene density (in 1 Mb windows). (<b>d</b>) Repeat density (in 1 Mb windows). (<b>e</b>) Density of single-nucleotide polymorphism used for genetic map construction (in 1 Mb windows). (<b>f</b>) Size of tandem gene arrays. Black blocks underneath show MRC regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Christopoulou, M. et al. Genome-wide architecture of disease resistance genes in Lettuce. G3 Genes Genomes Genetics 5, 2655–2669 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1615">31</a></sup>. The coloured lines in the centre show links between syntenic blocks of at least five genes derived from the most recent whole-genome triplication.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms14953/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Transposon content and annotation</h3><p>Through a combination of approaches we found that 1.8 Gb of the 2.38 Gb (74.2%) assembled genome of <i>L. sativa</i> comprised repetitive elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The most abundant elements were long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RT), specifically the <i>Gypsy</i> (33.9%) and <i>Copia</i> (24.9%) subfamilies. Besides the main group of LTR-RT elements 4.4% of the genome was annotated as Miniature Inverted-Repeat Transposable Elements and 1.2% as DNA elements, the remaining was classified into other repeat families or could not be assigned (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3172">Supplementary Data 5</a>). Repeat elements were distributed over all of the chromosomes; several chromosomes had internal regions with elevated densities of repeats, possibly indicative of centromeres but no major regions of heterochromatin were evident (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>, Track d).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene annotation</h3><p>A high confidence gene set of 38,919 gene models with good protein or EST support was constructed for <i>L. sativa</i> by merging gene models from different prediction pipelines (see Methods). These gene models have average coding lengths of 1.05 kb and 4.5 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3173">Supplementary Data 6</a>), similar to those in other sequenced plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). The average intergenic distance of 39.5 kb places <i>L. sativa</i> between <i>Nicotiana tomentosis</i> and <i>Capsicum annum</i> consistent with a direct correlation between intergenic distance and genome size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). Out of the total number of predicted genes, 29,681 (76.27%) genes had similarity to <i>Arabidopsis</i> TAIR10 annotations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3173">Supplementary Data 6</a>) and 28,951 (74.3%) were annotated using InterProScan5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1693">21</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3174">Supplementary Data 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167"> 8</a>). Annotation using KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27–30 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1704">22</a></sup> database yielded information for 7,553 <i>L. sativa</i> gene predictions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3175">Supplementary Data 9</a>). The combined data sets provided functional annotation for 31,348 (80.5%) gene models.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Small RNAs in lettuce and their target transcripts</h3><p>To identify microRNAs, loci generating phased, secondary siRNAs and other small RNAs, we sequenced 10 libraries each of small RNAs and uncapped or cleaved mRNAs (‘PARE’ libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhai, J. et al. MicroRNAs as master regulators of the plant NB-LRR defense gene family via the production of phased, trans-acting siRNAs. Genes Dev. 25, 2540–2553 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1723">23</a></sup>). Most of the small RNAs identified were 24-nt heterochromatic siRNAs as expected. In addition, there were a total of 86 miRNAs from 51 distinct miRNA families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3177">Supplementary Data 10</a>). Among these 51 miRNA families, we identified 21 novel miRNAs; 8 of these were 22 nucleotides in length and thus candidate triggers of secondary siRNAs, known as phased siRNAs or ‘phasiRNAs’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Fei, Q., Xia, R. &amp; Meyers, B. C. Phased, secondary, small interfering RNAs in posttranscriptional regulatory networks. Plant Cell 25, 2400–2415 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1730">24</a></sup> (produced from <i>PHAS</i> loci). Four of the 22-nt novel miRNAs, lsa-m1604, lsa-m5672, lsa-m1606 and lsa-m1057, were highly expressed. Combining the predicted miRNAs with the PARE data validated the cleavage of 288 miRNA target genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3178">Supplementary Data 11</a>). A total of 565 <i>PHAS</i> loci were identified by scanning the small RNAs against the lettuce genome, the majority (430) of which were annotated as protein-coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3179">Supplementary Data 12</a>). <i>NB-LRR</i> (NLR) resistance protein-encoding genes were the largest class of <i>PHAS</i> loci (246 genes), as in many other eudicots such as <i>Glycine max</i> and <i>Medicago truncatula</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Arikit, S. et al. An atlas of soybean small RNAs identifies phased siRNAs from hundreds of coding genes. Plant Cell 26, 4584–4601 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1762">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhai, J., Arikit, S., Simon, S. A., Kingham, B. F. &amp; Meyers, B. C. Rapid construction of Parallel Analysis of RNA End (PARE) libraries for Illumina sequencing. Methods 67, 84–90 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1765">26</a></sup>. The second largest class of <i>PHAS</i> loci (116 genes) was kinases, the highest number for kinases detected in all angiosperms analysed so far. The single miRNA trigger of these kinase phasiRNAs was predicted to be the novel miRNA, lsa-m5672. The lettuce-specific miRNA and high number of targets suggests a lineage-specific specialization of this post-transcriptional control of kinase genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Protein clustering and gene family analysis</h3><p>Clustering of the predicted proteome of lettuce using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Fischer, S. et al. Using OrthoMCL to assign proteins to OrthoMCL-DB groups or to cluster proteomes into new ortholog groups. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma Chapter 6, Unit 6.12.1-19. (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1780">27</a></sup> with proteins from nine other published plant genomes clustered 251,776 out of the 325,448 proteins predicted for all 10 species into 28,720 groups, which contained 29,511 (76%) of the lettuce proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Across the panel of 10 genomes, 1,475 ortholog groups had a member from each species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3180">Supplementary Data 13</a>). Calculations on this set of genes using <i>Vitis vinifera</i> as the reference revealed that Brassicaceae species have the highest rate of synonymous substitutions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1793">28</a></sup> (ds=<span class="stix">∼</span>4.53), followed by <i>Mimulus guttatus</i> (ds=3.78) and <i>L. sativa</i> (ds=3.12), while the tree species have the lowest ds values (<span class="stix">∼</span>1.65) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3181">Supplementary Data 14</a>). Most of the groups represented gene families that were shared between species (13,611 groups were present in more than one species and contained more than one gene per species).</p><p>Forty of 107 tested groups exhibited significant expansion or contractions in gene copy number across the panel of 10 species (see Methods) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3182">Supplementary Data 15</a>). Many (14 out of 40) had kinase-related activity. Of these 14 kinase-related groups, 8 had the receptor-like kinase (RLK) domain structure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Walker, J. C. Structure and function of the receptor-like protein kinases of higher plants. Plant Mol. Biol. 26, 1599–1609 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1813">29</a></sup>. From the total 696 RLKs annotated in lettuce, 655 were grouped into 204 groups; leucine-rich repeat-RLKs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Shiu, S.-H. &amp; Bleecker, A. B. Plant receptor-like kinase gene family: diversity, function, and signaling. Sci. Signal. 2001, re22 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1817">30</a></sup> are over-represented while the lectin-RLKs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Shiu, S.-H. &amp; Bleecker, A. B. Plant receptor-like kinase gene family: diversity, function, and signaling. Sci. Signal. 2001, re22 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1821">30</a></sup> are under-represented in lettuce compared to the other nine species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3180">Supplementary Data 13</a>). A total of 372 nucleotide-binding leucine-rich repeat receptor like (NLR) proteins in 31 groups were annotated in lettuce. Two groups that were present in all 10 genomes were significantly expanded in <i>L. sativa</i>, <i>Citrus sinensis</i>, <i>Populus tricocharpa</i> and <i>Theobroma cacao</i>. These include the <i>Resistance Gene Candidate 1</i> (<i>RGC1</i>) and <i>RGC21</i> subfamilies in <i>L. sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Christopoulou, M. et al. Genome-wide architecture of disease resistance genes in Lettuce. G3 Genes Genomes Genetics 5, 2655–2669 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1853">31</a></sup>. Another 30 NLR groups varied in copy number and presence/absence across species. Two of these groups were significantly expanded in lettuce compared to the other genomes; one encompassed the <i>RGC16</i> subfamily, which is the biggest NLR family present in lettuce<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Christopoulou, M. et al. Genome-wide architecture of disease resistance genes in Lettuce. G3 Genes Genomes Genetics 5, 2655–2669 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1860">31</a></sup>. In contrast, 10 NLR groups showed expansions in other plant genomes but were single or low copy in lettuce. There were four NLR groups that are specific to <i>L. sativa</i> at the stringencies used (see Methods) and together contain 141 NLR proteins, with the two biggest containing mostly <i>RGC12</i> and <i>RGC4</i> members. Considering all 28,720 clusters, 1,617 clusters containing 7,035 genes (18% of annotated genes) were detected as specific to <i>L. sativa</i> at the threshold used (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3180">Supplementary Data 13</a>). These <i>L. sativa</i>-specific clusters showed a significant enrichment of GO terms related to ‘Signaling’ (GO:0023052) and ‘Response to stimulus’ (GO:0050896) with ‘Defense response’ (GO:0006952) (Fisher exact test and FDR corrected <i>P</i>-value 1.5 e<sup>−75</sup>) showing the greatest enrichment (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3183">Supplementary Data 16</a>). Expansions of NLR genes have been the result of local duplications since 83% of sequences predicted to encode NLRs are within tandem arrays. These data are consistent with higher evolutionary selection pressure for diversification in <i>L. sativa</i> of some families of resistance genes compared to other species. In contrast, genes encoding disease response-related proteins such as the Lipoxygenase (LOX) gene family show greater conservation with no expansion at the family level and the structure of the family is conserved across all genomes analysed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>).</p><p>Analysis of Pfam protein domains revealed expansion and diversification of two families related to latex and rubber production. Seventy-eight genes belonging to the Bet_v_1/Major Latex Protein (PF00407/IPR000916) family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Oh, S. K. et al. Isolation, characterization, and functional analysis of a novel cDNA clone encoding a small rubber particle protein from Hevea brasiliensis. J. Biol. Chem. 274, 17132–17138 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1901">32</a></sup> were found in <i>L. sativa</i>; this is significantly more than the average of 34 present in the other nine genomes studied (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3184">Supplementary Data 17</a>). Precise function(s) of these proteins is not known but they are present in latex and involved in responses biotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Van Loon, L. C., Rep, M. &amp; Pieterse, C. M. J. Significance of inducible defense-related proteins in infected plants. Annu. Rev. Phytopathol. 44, 135–162 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1911">33</a></sup>. The Rubber Elongation Factor (REF) (PF05755) family is also expanded in <i>L. sativa</i> with 11 members compared to an average 3.6 of paralogs per genome in the non-rubber producing species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3184">Supplementary Data 17</a>). This family is involved in biosynthesis of rubber in <i>Hevea brasiliensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Oh, S. K. et al. Isolation, characterization, and functional analysis of a novel cDNA clone encoding a small rubber particle protein from Hevea brasiliensis. J. Biol. Chem. 274, 17132–17138 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1924">32</a></sup> and its copy number is related to the overall capability to produce rubber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Tang, C. et al. The rubber tree genome reveals new insights into rubber production and species adaptation. Nat. Plants 2, 16073 (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1928">34</a></sup>. <i>H. brasiliensis</i> has 18 <i>REF/SRPP’s</i>, the majority of which are in a single cluster<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Tang, C. et al. The rubber tree genome reveals new insights into rubber production and species adaptation. Nat. Plants 2, 16073 (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1939">34</a></sup>. Similarly, in <i>L. sativa</i> that also produces high-quality rubber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Bushman, B. S. et al. Identification and comparison of natural rubber from two Lactuca species. Phytochemistry 67, 2590–2596 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d2878e1946">35</a></sup> the major portion of this family is located as a tandem array of eight members located in LG9 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). Therefore, this family is expanded in both of these taxonomically distinct, rubber-producing species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genome triplication</h3><p>Comparisons of genome structure of the published plant genomes and <i>L. sativa</i> against <i>V. vinifera as</i> a reference showed that chromosome conservation correlated with growth habit and generation time based on the size of the syntenic blocks as expected (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3185">Supplementary Data 18</a>). Species with long generation times or vegetatively propagated (<i>T. cacao</i>, <i>Solanum tuberosum</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>C. sinensis</i>) were more syntenic than those with faster life cycles (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Brassica rapa</i>). The extent of detectable synteny also correlates with the ploidy level. <i>T. cacao</i> (2<i>n</i>) exhibits more synteny with V. vinifera (2<i>n</i>) than <i>A. thaliana</i> (8<i>n</i>) and <i>B. rapa</i> (12<i>n</i>). Consistent with this, <i>L. sativa</i> (6<i>n</i>) showed an intermediate level of synteny to <i>V. vinifera</i>. Visualization of the syntenic hits between these two genomes suggested a whole-genome triplication event in lettuce since divergence from the grape lineage. <i>V. vinifera</i> chromosomes 1,3,9,11,15,16 and 17 are replicated almost entirely in three different locations in <i>L. sativa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Multiple polyploidization events have been described in different Asterid orders (Lamiales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Aagaard, J. E., Olmstead, R. G., Willis, J. H. &amp; Phillips, P. C. Duplication of floral regulatory genes in the Lamiales. Am. J. Bot. 92, 1284–1293 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2030">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ibarra-Laclette, E. et al. Architecture and evolution of a minute plant genome. Nature 498, 94–98 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2033">37</a></sup>, Solanales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2038">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Qin, C. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of cultivated and wild peppers provides insights into Capsicum domestication and specialization. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci 111, 5135–5140 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2041">39</a></sup>, Ericales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Huang, S. et al. Draft genome of the kiwifruit Acti nidia chinensis. Nat. Commun. 4, 2640 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2045">40</a></sup>, Apiales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 48, 657–666 (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2049">41</a></sup> and Asterales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Barker, M. S. et al. Multiple paleopolyploidizations during the evolution of the Compositae reveal parallel patterns of duplicate gene retention after millions of years. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 2445–2455 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2053">42</a></sup>). Analysis of mutation rates between pairs of syntelogs are consistent with whole-genome duplication events in the Solanaceae and Lamiaceae that are independent of the event basal to Compositae family, after separation of these Asterid families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). All these events correlate with phylogenetic relationships between the species based on CEG gene sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2060">19</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). The divergence between <i>L. sativa</i> and the six Lamiid species is similar (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>); higher similarity was found to <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> (kiwi fruit; Ericaceae), as expected as this species is basal to all Euasterids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Bremer, B. et al. Phylogenetics of asterids based on 3 coding and 3 non-coding chloroplast DNA markers and the utility of non-coding DNA at higher taxonomic levels. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 24, 274–301 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2077">43</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Detection of ancient poliploydization events."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Detection of ancient poliploydization events.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms14953/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms14953/MediaObjects/41467_2017_Article_BFncomms14953_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms14953/MediaObjects/41467_2017_Article_BFncomms14953_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="539"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Syntenic dotplot of <i>L. sativa</i> versus <i>V. vinifera</i> (<i>x</i> axis: <i>L</i>. <i>sativa</i> chromosomes; <i>y</i> axis: <i>V. vinifera</i> chromosomes). (<b>b</b>) Density distribution of estimated synonymous substitution rate (ds) of syntelog pairs for intragenomic comparisons and for <i>L. sativa</i> against a panel of Asterid species; inserts are enlargements of the main plot. (<b>c</b>) RAxML phylogenetic tree of the Asterid clade; scale is estimated nucleotide substitutions per site. Numbers represent inferred positions of the whole-genome duplication/triplication events observed in the syntelogs data (<b>b</b>). (<b>d</b>) Distributions of triplicated paralogous genes within the lettuce genome. Chromosomal pseudomolecules are arranged progressively along the three axes: <i>x</i>: LG1, LG4, LG7; <i>y</i>: LG2, LG5, LG8; <i>z</i>: LG3, LG6, LG9. WGT, whole-genome triplication.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms14953/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Analysis of the intragenomic collinearity in <i>L. sativa</i> provided further strong evidence that a large proportion of the genome is present in triplicate (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Analysis of the distribution of colinear blocks comprising at least five genes detected 10 separate genomic regions that are present in triplicate in the <i>L. sativa</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Synonymous substitution rates between syntelogs in the triplicated regions differed between the three regions, suggesting two events leading the triplicated state. These may have involved a whole-genome duplication followed by subsequent hybridization to a related diploid species and then genome duplication of the triploid shortly thereafter as has been proposed for <i>B. rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035–1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2168">44</a></sup> rather than a single event leading to the triplicated state. Analysis of substitution rates also placed these events 40–45 Myr, soon after the estimated origin of the family 50 Myr (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Funk, V. A. et al. in Plant Diversity and Complexity Patterns: Local, Regional, and Global Dimensions: Proc. Int. Symp. Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters in Copenhagen, Denmark, 25–28 May 2003, Vol. 55, (eds Friis, I. &amp; Balslev, H.) 343–374 (Kgl. Danske Videnskabernes Selskab, 2005)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2172">1</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3186">Supplementary Data 19</a>). Divergence rates were used to assign the paralogous regions as products of the first and second duplication events. Although individual paralogous regions varied, overall the three subgenomes detected were similar in total size, repeat content and number of genes; there was no evidence for genomic changes specific to one subgenome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Table 6</a>).</p><p>Altogether, the detected triplicated regions cover at least 651 Mb of the <i>L. sativa</i> genome (26% of the assembly) and contain 11,816 (30%) of all predicted genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Table 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). Of the genes within these regions 2,912 (25%) have retained at least one syntenic counterpart. Some types of genes were enriched in triplicated regions; these included genes encoding transcription factors and DNA-binding proteins as well as components of the nucleus, nucleosome, membranes and cell wall. These transcription factors contain a wide set of functional domains (AP2, Homeobox, KNOX, WRKY, TCP) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3187">Supplementary Data 20</a>). Of 54 TCP transcription factors, 27 are present in a triplicated region (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>); this family includes <i>Cycloidea</i>-like genes that have been implicated in the development of the elaborate compound flowers characteristic of the Compositae family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Chapman, M. A., Leebens-Mack, J. H. &amp; Burke, J. M. Positive selection and expression divergence following gene duplication in the sunflower Cycloidea gene family. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 1260–1273 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2204">45</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Genome-wide distribution of triplicated regions detected in L. sativa relative to genomic features and specific gene families."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Genome-wide distribution of triplicated regions detected in <i>L. sativa</i> relative to genomic features and specific gene families.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms14953/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms14953/MediaObjects/41467_2017_Article_BFncomms14953_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms14953/MediaObjects/41467_2017_Article_BFncomms14953_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="948"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Each plot represents a single chromosomal pseudomolecule showing the location of the different triplicated regions represented as coloured bars. Repeat, genic and exonic densities are displayed above each chromosomal bar. Due to the large differences in scale, repeat content is scaled on the left axis and genic/exonic content is called on the right axis. Directly below the chromosomal bar are the MRC regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Christopoulou, M. et al. Genome-wide architecture of disease resistance genes in Lettuce. G3 Genes Genomes Genetics 5, 2655–2669 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2224">31</a></sup>. The lower four tracks show the distribution of different types of genes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms14953/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The patterns of retention and diversification differed between families of transcription factors. The nine <i>Cycloidea</i>-like genes in lettuce contain the TCP domain, have similarity to genes in other Compositae species and form a tight clade within the TCP family (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). Of these, seven are present within the triplicated regions and five have retained syntenic copies. The WRKY family of transcription factors also shows retention of duplicate copies; 32 are present within the triplicated regions and 21 are in pairs or triplets. Clustering of all the WRKY proteins in the genome showed no particular trend in retention across the tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3167">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>), except as expected syntelog pairs (or triplets) were clustered in most cases. Analysis at the sequence level of these pairs did not provide evidence of diversifying selection; however, the 3′ end was more conserved than the 5′ end with two- to seven-fold differences in pairwise sequence divergence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3188">Supplementary Data 21</a>). This is consistent with selection for conservation of the WRKY domain present in the 3′ end while allowing divergence of the 5′ end potentially leading to gain of functions. Similar analysis of the AP2 family of transcription factors did not detect such differences between the 3′ and 5′ ends (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3188">Supplementary Data 21</a>). Expression analysis revealed differences in expression of syntelogs; only 3 out of the 12 WRKY and only 3 out of 23 AP2 syntelog pairs were co-expressed based in a Weighted Gene Correlation-Expression Network Analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#MOESM3189">Supplementary Data 22</a>). Syntelogs were not detected for some genes encoding transcription factor families. The proportion of genes encoding MADS proteins within the triplicated regions (30%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>) is similar to those encoding AP2 (40%) and WRKY (33%) proteins; however, only 6% are detected as syntenic pairs. Since these genes are still present within a syntenic block significant divergence at the sequence level between the paralogs is the likely cause for the lack of detected synteny.</p><p>Some genes are under-represented in the detected triplicated regions. Genes encoding proteins related to defence response, signal transduction, protein kinase activity and protein phosphorylation activity are less prevalent in the triplicated regions; only 29 out of 437 NB-LRR candidate genes and 3 out of 21 LOX genes were detected within the triplicated regions. The distribution of these types of genes may reflect the genomic landscape where they are located rather than gene loss itself. MRCs are generally in gene-poor, more dynamic areas with higher repeat content compared to the rest of the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Christopoulou, M. et al. Genome-wide architecture of disease resistance genes in Lettuce. G3 Genes Genomes Genetics 5, 2655–2669 (2015)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2265">31</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms14953#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). Rapid evolution, higher repeat content and genome rearrangements would cause loss of detectable synteny.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>This genome assembly of lettuce is one of the more complete for any plant species reported so far, particularly for genomes larger than 2 Gb with a high repeat content. The genome size of <i>L. sativa</i> is typical of many Compositae species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Garcia, S. et al. Recent updates and developments to plant genome size databases. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1159–D1166 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2284">46</a></sup>, despite the family having rapid-cycling weedy species. It provides the first high-quality, genetically validated, reference genome for analysis of the highly successful Compositae family.</p><p>The Dovetail technology greatly increased the contiguity of the assembly, assimilated additional scaffolds into chromosomal pseudomolecules, identified chimeric scaffolds that had been missed by genetic analysis, and oriented and ordered scaffolds through complex regions. The Dovetail approach was able to accommodate the high repeat content of the lettuce genome. However, more than half of the remaining scaffolds (6,026) are under 2 kb and predominantly contain repeated sequences; because the HiRise algorithm requires unique alignments of both read pairs such scaffolds are not captured by HiRise. Only a small amount of sequencing was specifically required. The only major requirement is the ability to extract sufficiently large DNA. Therefore, it is readily applicable to numerous species for which there are no or only partial genome assemblies. Consequently, it is likely to result in the rapid increase in the availability of high-quality genome assemblies for numerous non-model species.</p><p>The whole-genome triplication event detected in lettuce is basal to the Asterales and distinct from the triplication event recently reported for <i>Daucus carrota</i> (carrot) in the Apiales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 48, 657–666 (2016)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2297">41</a></sup>, which is also in the Euasterid II clade. Interestingly, these events seem to have occurred simultaneously in common with many lineages at or shortly after the Cretaceous–Paleocene transition (66 Myr), at which time <span class="stix">∼</span>75% of all species became extinct<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334–1347 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2301">47</a></sup>. This is consistent with polyploidy resulting in evolutionary innovations and phenotypic plasticity that conferred selective advantages for successful colonization of disturbed habitats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Fawcett, J. A., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the Cretaceous-Tertiary extinction event. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. 106, 5737–5742 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2305">48</a></sup>. As in whole-genome duplications of other plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97–100 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2309">49</a></sup> and animals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Blomme, T. et al. The gain and loss of genes during 600 million years of vertebrate evolution. Genome Biol. 7, R43 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2314">50</a></sup>, genes encoding some but not all transcription factors were enriched in triplicated regions of the lettuce genome. Patterns of sequence divergence indicated subfunctionalization at the 5′ end of WRKY genes, and possible neo-functionalization of the AP2 and MADS genes. These changes in regulatory machinery could have led to new phenotypes that would have enabled members of the family to adapt to new environments.</p><p>Other genomic features that may have contributed to the success of the family include diversification of <i>Cycloidea</i>-like genes that may be involved in formation of the complex capitulum (a composite of many flowers); such an elaborate reproductive organ may be more attractive to pollinators. The Compositae are renowned for their diversity of secondary metabolites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Seaman, F. C. Sesquiterpene lactones as taxonomic characters in the Asteraceae. Bot. Rev. 48, 121–594 (1982)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2324">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Oh, M.-M., Trick, H. N. &amp; Rajashekar, C. B. Secondary metabolism and antioxidants are involved in environmental adaptation and stress tolerance in Lettuce. J. Plant Physiol. 166, 180–191 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms14953#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d2878e2327">52</a></sup>. Genes involved in the production of latex that gives lettuce its characteristic milky sap were expanded and may be involved in defence against biotic stresses. It is also interesting that there are over 20 novel miRNA, one of which targets kinase transcripts; this may be indicative of another level of regulatory innovation; it will be informative to analyse the miRNA repertoires of other Compositae species.</p><p>This high-quality reference genome provides the foundation for syntenic inferences across the Compositae.
